              <div class="section">
               <div class="review nosyndicate" id="review_185373497" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
                <link href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/185373497" itemprop="url"/>
                <a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/764994-rick" title="Rick">
                 <img alt="Rick" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1289746453p2/764994.jpg"/>
                </a>
                <div class="left bodycol">
                 <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
                  <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/185373497?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">
                   Jul 15, 2011
                  </a>
                  <a class="user" href="/user/show/764994-rick" title="Rick">
                   Rick
                  </a>
                  rated it
                  <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">
                   4 of 5 stars
                  </a>
                  <span class="greyText">
                   
                  </span>
                  <a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/8909051-everything-flows" title="ebook 9781590173893">
                   review of another edition
                  </a>
                  <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
                   Shelves:
                   <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/764994-rick?shelf=fiction">
                    fiction
                   </a>
                  </div>
                 </div>
                 <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer185373497" style="">
                   <span id="freeTextContainer9799845880412330320">
                    But you know you cant outrun the history train. --Paul Simon.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    Vassily Grossmans last work, begun in 1955 and left uncompleted at his death in 1964, is part novel and part history. Ivan Grigoryevich has spent 30 years in the gulag when he is released in the wake of Stalins death and struggles to return to a life of freedom and normality. He is the protagonist of the novel. His chapters alternate and in some cases initiate the historical essays on topics related to the Soviets system of terr
                   </span>
                   <span id="freeText9799845880412330320" style="display:none">
                    But you know you cant outrun the history train.  --Paul Simon.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    Vassily Grossmans last work, begun in 1955 and left uncompleted at his death in 1964, is part novel and part history. Ivan Grigoryevich has spent 30 years in the gulag when he is released in the wake of Stalins death and struggles to return to a life of freedom and normality. He is the protagonist of the novel. His chapters alternate and in some cases initiate the historical essays on topics related to the Soviets system of terrorisminformers, initial arrests and interrogation, women in the gulag, Lenins culpability in Soviet state terrorism, Stalins attack on science, and his intentions to launch an purge of Jewish doctors and intellectuals, one that would have netted Grossman had Stalin survived to carry it out. The sections with Ivan, even without the drama of war, are as compelling as Grossmans great war novel, Life and Fate, and while the essay-like sections are compelling in their own way the overall effect for the reader is a little like jumping between drafts for a novel and the research notes behind the novelexcept that the notes are a very high order of journalism.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    We follow Ivan after his release as he adjusts to a life of restored freedom, wondering Who is guilty? Who will be held responsible? Wondering, too, where he can be and what he can do. His true love from before his arrest has moved on. His cousin is embarrassed by him, afraid of his judgments, resentful of the imagined moral superiority he assigns to Ivan. Instead Nikolay totes up all his invisible resistances and whitewashes all his go along to get along betrayals of principle and colleague. Ivan, however, knows that everyone is complicit and that the terrors most evil impact is the evil it compels others to do.
                    <br/>
                    <br/>
                    The essays are blunt in their indictment of Stalinism and those who drove it: But you can look all you likeyou wont find anyone who admits to being guilty. What they did, they did for everyone, Grossman writes of the forced and enforced famine that killed millions, Thats why they drove mothers to eat their own children. Despite the horrors of the long decades between 1920 and 1956, both parts share a hard-won, even defiant optimism: The history of humanity is the history of human freedom. The growth of human potentiality is expressed in the growth of freedom. Freedom is not, as Engles claimed, the recognition of necessity. Freedom is the direct opposite of necessity; freedom is necessity overcome. In one slim volume, Grossman equals, even surpasses, what Solzhenitsyn accomplished in A Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich and The Gulag Archipelego. He proves that history isnt always written by the victors, sometimes its written by the survivors.
                   </span>
                   <a data-text-id="9799845880412330320" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">
                    ...more
                   </a>
                  </span>

                 <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer37140458" style="">
                   <span id="freeTextContainer17108665218503559029">
                    Quin y donde nos escondi al gran narrador que es Vassili Grossman? Por qu motivo los medios no acogieron sus obras con el apoyo que se merecan? Tal vez su defensa de la libertad y sus opiniones contra el error/horror en que se convirti la dictadura comunista en Rusia? No lo se, pero desde luego nos han privado de conocer antes a un MAGNFICO autor.
                    <br/>
                    Entre novela y ensayo poltico sobre la libertad y su vulneracin, lejos de la gran distancia de VIDA Y DESTINO, pero con un contenido poltic
                   </span>
                   <span id="freeText17108665218503559029" style="display:none">
                    Quin y donde nos escondi al gran narrador que es Vassili Grossman? Por qu motivo los medios no acogieron sus obras con el apoyo que se merecan? Tal vez su defensa de la libertad y sus opiniones contra el error/horror en que se convirti la dictadura comunista en Rusia? No lo se, pero desde luego nos han privado de conocer antes a un MAGNFICO autor.
                    <br/>
                    Entre novela y ensayo poltico sobre la libertad y su vulneracin, lejos de la gran distancia de VIDA Y DESTINO, pero con un contenido poltico ms intenso y claro. La parte narrativa, fluctuante como un ro, da paso a claras reflexiones desde dentro del "sistema" sovitico que el paso de los aos han confirmado. Y todo ello, tomando partido por la libertad del ser humano como nica causa digna de ser tenida en cuenta. Y sealando de manera clara e inequvoca cmo la Revolucin se convirti en una dictadura impuesta por el terror con mtodos tan expeditivos como los utilizados por los nazis: el genocidio, el exterminio intencionado de millones de personas.
                    <br/>
                    En fin, impactante, rotundo, dramtico... vital. Me ha encantado.
                   </span>

                    Incredible. This is perhaps the best work I've read on the origins and consequences of dictatorship in the Soviet Union. It's all the more powerful for being concise - a must-read for anyone interested in understanding the driving force behind the continuation (and perfection) of absolutism in Russia under Lenin and Stalin, and its horrible significance for humanity.

                  <a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/8722251-everything-flows" title="Hardcover 9781846552366">
                   review of another edition
                  </a>
                  <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
                   Shelves:
                   <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5884970-kevin-tole?shelf=classics">
                    classics
                   </a>

                   <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5884970-kevin-tole?shelf=russian">
                    russian

                 <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer492869705" style="">
                   <span id="freeTextContainer11656836966744047872">
                    This is a book which everyone should read. Both better and worse than
                    <em>
                     Life and Fate
                    </em>
                    . Is this fiction? Or is it just the method for Grossman to announce his views and discoveries. and does that really matter? Quite a staggering piece of writing containing two of the most brutally honest chapters I have ever read. These two chapters alone should be read by everyone. I learnt more about the history of Stalinist Russia in particular - but also of Russia and Russian ethos and history than anything by
                   </span>
                   <span id="freeText11656836966744047872" style="display:none">
                    This is a book which everyone should read. Both better and worse than
                    <em>
                     Life and Fate
                    </em>
                    . Is this fiction? Or is it just the method for Grossman to announce his views and discoveries. and does that really matter? Quite a staggering piece of writing containing two of the most brutally honest chapters I have ever read. These two chapters alone should be read by everyone. I learnt more about the history of Stalinist Russia in particular - but also of Russia and Russian ethos and history than anything by Robert Service or Orlando Figes or Marx or Tolstoy. Grossman reads like Dostoyevsky in the 20th century. I found it illuminating on both Russian ethos and on Marxist Leninism as a product of Russian character. It ought to be read by everone. There. I've said it three times. Maybe it will happen now.
                   </span>

              <div class="section">
               <div class="review" id="review_549378310" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
                <link href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/549378310" itemprop="url"/>
                <a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15611044-neil-randall" title="Neil Randall">
                 <img alt="Neil Randall" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1355906626p2/15611044.jpg"/>
                </a>
                <div class="left bodycol">
                 <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
                  <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/549378310?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">
                   Mar 01, 2013
                  </a>
                  <a class="user" href="/user/show/15611044-neil-randall" title="Neil Randall">
                   Neil Randall
                  </a>
                  rated it
                  <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">
                   5 of 5 stars
                  </a>
                  <span class="greyText">
                   
                  </span>
                  <a class="lightGreyText" href="/book/show/8722251-everything-flows" title="Hardcover 9781846552366">
                   review of another edition
                  </a>
                 </div>
                 <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer549378310" style="">
                   <span id="freeTextContainer9658409402899882122">
                    Grossman's 'Everything Flows', albeit unfinished, is a very powerful and uncompromising piece of writing. After Stalin's death, the protagonist of the story, Ivan, returns to Moscow after 30 years in the hard labour camps. There he visits family and friends, all of whom are trying to assess their own collective guilt. Some are informers, other just normal people who don't quite know why they were lucky enough to be spared Ivan's fate. The novel contains several different strands, some novelistic
                   </span>
                   <span id="freeText9658409402899882122" style="display:none">
                    Grossman's 'Everything Flows', albeit unfinished, is a very powerful and uncompromising piece of writing. After Stalin's death, the protagonist of the story, Ivan, returns to Moscow after 30 years in the hard labour camps. There he visits family and friends, all of whom are trying to assess their own collective guilt. Some are informers, other just normal people who don't quite know why they were lucky enough to be spared Ivan's fate. The novel contains several different strands, some novelistic, others historical and philosophical. Most striking is the account of the Ukrainian famine of the early 1920's - harrowing stuff. Essential reading.
                   </span>
                   <a data-text-id="9658409402899882122" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">
                    ...more
                   </a>
                  </span>

             <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
              <div class="section">
               <div class="review" id="review_963339253" itemprop="reviews" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
                <link href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/963339253" itemprop="url"/>
                <a class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457963-chuck-lopresti" title="Chuck LoPresti">
                 <img alt="Chuck LoPresti" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/users/1308763901p2/2457963.jpg"/>
                </a>
                <div class="left bodycol">
                 <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
                  <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/963339253?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate">
                   Jun 10, 2014
                  </a>
                  <a class="user" href="/user/show/2457963-chuck-lopresti" title="Chuck LoPresti">
                   Chuck LoPresti
                  </a>
                  rated it
                  <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">
                   5 of 5 stars
                  </a>
                 </div>
                 <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <span class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer963339253" style="">
                   <span id="freeTextContainer8560134005617004681">
                    A book that everyone should read. It doesn't take long at near 200 pages, about the length of Shalomov's Kolyma Tales or Platonov's Foundation Pit but it will sit in your mind much longer than that. I imagine most readers will at some point throw it down in horror and then use those empty hands to help hold up their leaking heads. The atrocities describes here may not be new to those familiar with the above mentioned texts or Grossman's Hell of Treblinka. But if you're not familiar with these -
                   </span>
                   <span id="freeText8560134005617004681" style="display:none">
                    A book that everyone should read. It doesn't take long at near 200 pages, about the length of Shalomov's Kolyma Tales or Platonov's Foundation Pit but it will sit in your mind much longer than that. I imagine most readers will at some point throw it down in horror and then use those empty hands to help hold up their leaking heads. The atrocities describes here may not be new to those familiar with the above mentioned texts or Grossman's Hell of Treblinka. But if you're not familiar with these - be warned - you are in for intense pain. If you can finish this with out sobbing in horror or randomly asking the skies how it might have happened you are tougher than me. You won't be asking how it happened when you're done because Grossman has detailed the process from Lenin to lime pits with this powerful indictment of the horrors of early 20th-Century Soviet genocide. This book should also be noted for the fairly concise handling of the subject matter and they way I see it - no student should graduate post secondary education without having read this - Life and Fate is about 600 pages longer. Grossman balances the details of the people that enabled, executed and then evaded taking responsibility for their actions. US readers might want to take note that when he's talking about the "West" he is seemingly not talking about America where slavery also enabled development in many circumstances - but that's a debate for another place. Know that you will witness what we can only hope is the limit of human cruelty. Grossman offers little but some hope that humans can do better, that there's a way to have progress that doesn't require that slaves be fed to the pernicious beast of mechanical progress. It's easy to feel Grossman pleading with the reader to learn from what has happened. Grossman is a great thinker and his prose is appropriately simple for this work. He writes as well if not a bit better than Shalomov but doesn't come close to the prose of his friend Platonov. I can't imagine anyone reads this for pleasure or pure literary appreciation - rather this is a scholarly and unflinching look at how genocide happens. Read it then gather your children and go for a walk. You'll need it.
                   </span>
                   <a data-text-id="8560134005617004681" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">
                    ...more
                   </a>
                  </span>
                 </div>

      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('01CqDEEY5LGoWeP+EWkNxqrHUAfusFr7w7KbkHM04K7gs/RuTDew/FgnygmuTJXCbvF3PW2T3rbATaobKuHj1A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ni+pGY9fypSmSisMCSBBEYihoRKCMFXXwjkSZmJZHPgFzPd7gnCe2VY0Avu2BdkVTJeGKAET0ZrBxiPtO4wfgg==')})
  }
               </script>

(What, after all, is your run-of-the-mill scribbler of stories if not an accomplished corruptor of conceptions?) The logic of the club is strict and uncompromising. Every Saturday, members meet in a firelit room filled with empty black bookshelves where they strive to top one another by developing ever unlikelier, ever more perfect conceptions: a rehearsal of Hamlet hijacked by an actor who vanishes with the role; the double life of a merry medieval cleric derailed by a costume change; a machine-run world that imprisons mens minds while conscripting their bodies; a dead Roman scribe stranded this side of the River Acheron. But in this book set in an ominous Soviet Moscow of the 1920s, the members of the club are strangely mistrustful of one another, while all are under the spell of its despotic President, and there is Shortly before the outbreak of World War II, he is sent to an obscure village with one week\'s ration of canned meat and orders to guard a downed plane. Apparently forgotten by his unit, Chonkin resumes his life as a peasant and passes the war tending the village postmistress\'s garden. (The Guardian)Set in a remote Siberian depot immediately following the demolition of one of the gulags notorious camps and the emancipation of its prisoners, Faithful Ruslan is an embittered cri de coeur from a writer whose circumstances obliged him to resist the violence of arbitrary power. Every writer who writes anything in this country is made to feel he has committed a crime, Georgi Vladimov said. Dissident, he said, is a word that they force on you. His mother, a victim of Stalins anti-Semitic policy, had been interred for two years in one of the camps from which Vladimov derived the wrenching detail of Faithful Ruslan. The novel circulated in samizdat for more than a decade, often attributed to Solzhenitsyn, before its publication in the West led to Vladimovs harassment and exile. A starving stray, tortured and abandoned by the godlike Master whom he has unconditionally loved, Ruslan and his cadre of fellow guard dogs dutifully wait for the arrival of new prisonersbut the unexpected arrival of a work party provokes a climactic bloodletting. Fashioned from the perceptions of an uncomprehending animal, Vladimovs insistently ironic indictment of the gulag spirals to encompass all of Mans inexplicable cruelty.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a It presents the contents of her two previous collections, On the Golden Porch and Sleepwalker in a Fog, along with several previously uncollected stories. Tolstaya writes of lonely children and lost love, of philosophers of the absurd and poets working as janitors, of angels and halfwits. She shows how the extraordinary will suddenly erupt in the midst of ordinary life, as she explores the human condition with a matchless combination of unbound imagination and unapologetic Using his own experiences as a journalist and propagandis&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText8630334381076974376\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;One of the great masterpieces of Russian literature, the Red Cavalry cycle retains today the shocking freshness that made Babel\'s reputation when the stories were first published in the 1920s. Using his own experiences as a journalist and propagandist with the Red Army during the war against Poland, Babel brings to life an astonishing cast of characters from the exuberant, violent era of early Soviet history: commissars and colonels, Cossacks and peasants, and among them the bespectacled, Jewish writer/intellectual, observing it all and trying to establish his role in the new Russia.\n\n\nDrawn from the acclaimed, award-winning Complete Works of Isaac Babel, this volume includes all of the Red Cavalry cycle; Babel\'s 1920 diary, from which the material for the fiction was drawn; and his preliminary sketches for the storiesthe whole constituting a fascinating picture of a great writer turning life into art.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a data-text-id=\"8630334381076974376\" The newly married Dostoyevskys, Fyodor, and his wife, Anna Grigor\'yevna, are on their way to Germany, for a four-year trip. This is not, like J. M. Dostoyevsky\'s reckless passions for gambling, for his literary vocation, for his wife, are matched by her all-forgiving love, which in turn resonates with the love of literature\'s disciple, Leonid Tsypkin, for Dostoyevsky. In a remarkable introductory essay (which appeared in The New Yorker), Susan Sontag explains why it is something of a miracle that Summer in Baden-Baden has survived, and celebrates the happy event of        <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor">
        <div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer">
         <h2 class="brownBackground">
          <a href="/author/show/19595.Vasily_Grossman">
           About Vasily Grossman
          </a>
         </h2>
        </div>
        <div class="bigBoxBody">
         <div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <a href="/author/show/19595.Vasily_Grossman">
           <img alt="19595" class="right" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1391607075p2/19595.jpg"/>
          </a>
          <div class="readable">
           <span id="freeTextContainer16248379574620616297">
            :  
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Born Iosif Solomonovich Grossman into an emancipated Jewish family, he did not receive a traditional Jewish education. A Russian nanny turned his name Yossya into Russian Vasya (a diminutive of Vasily), which was accepted by the whole family. His father had social-democratic convictions and joined the Mensheviks. Young Vasily Grossman idealistically supported the Russian Revo
           </span>
           <span id="freeText16248379574620616297" style="display:none">
            :  
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Born Iosif Solomonovich Grossman into an emancipated Jewish family, he did not receive a traditional Jewish education. A Russian nanny turned his name Yossya into Russian Vasya (a diminutive of Vasily), which was accepted by the whole family. His father had social-democratic convictions and joined the Mensheviks. Young Vasily Grossman idealistically supported the Russian Revolution of 1917.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            When the Great Patriotic War broke out in 1941, Grossman's mother was trapped in Berdichev by the invading German army, and eventually murdered together with 20,000 to 30,000 other Jews who did not evacuate Berdychiv. Grossman was exempt from military service, but volunteered for the front, where he spent more than 1,000 days. He became a war reporter for the popular Red Army newspaper Krasnaya Zvezda (Red Star). As the war raged on, he covered its major events, including the Battle of Moscow, the Battle of Stalingrad, the Battle of Kursk, and the Battle of Berlin. In addition to war journalism, his novels (such as The People are Immortal ( )) were being published in newspapers and he came to be regarded as a legendary war hero. The novel Stalingrad (1950), later renamed For a Just Cause (  ), is based on his own experiences during the siege.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Grossman's descriptions of ethnic cleansing in Ukraine and Poland, and the liberation of the Treblinka and Majdanek extermination camps, were some of the first eyewitness accounts as early as 1943of what later became known as the Holocaust. His article The Hell of Treblinka[1] 1944) was disseminated at the Nuremberg War Crimes Tribunal as evidence for the prosecution.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Grossman died of stomach cancer in 1964, not knowing whether his novels would ever be read by the public.
           </span>
           <a data-text-id="16248379574620616297" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">
            ...more
           </a>
          </div>
          <a class="actionLink moreLink" href="/author/show/19595.Vasily_Grossman">
           More about Vasily Grossman...
          </a>

        <div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer">
         <h2 class="brownBackground">
          <a href="/work/quotes/728857">
           Quotes from Forever Flowing
          </a>
         </h2>
        </div>
        <div class="bigBoxBody">
         <div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
           <span class="readable">
            Ivan tells Anna: "I used to imagine that being embraced by a woman . . . as something so wonderful that it would make me forget everthing . . . [But] happiness, it turns out, will be to share with you the burden I can't share with anyone else.
           </span>
           <nobr>
            
            <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/239967-ivan-tells-anna-i-used-to-imagine-that-being-embraced">
             16 likes
            </a>
           </nobr>
           <div class="clear">
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
           <span class="readable">
            I used to think freedom was freedom of speech, freedom of the press, freedom of conscience. But freedom is the whole life of everyone. Here is what it amounts to: you have to have the right to sow what you wish to, to make shoes or coats, to bake into bread the flour ground from the grain you have sown, and to sell it or not sell it as you wish; for the lathe operator, the steelworker, and the artist its a matter of being able to live as you wish and work as you wish and not as they order you to. And in our country there is no freedom  not for those who write books nor for those who sow grain nor for those who make shoes. (Grossman, p. 99) He noted that In peoples day-to-day struggle to live, in the extreme efforts workers put forth to earn an extra ruble through moonlighting, in the collective farmers battle for bread and potatoes as the one and only fruit of their labor, he [Ivan Grigoryevich] could sense more than the desire to live better, to fill ones childrens stomachs and to clothe them. In the battle for the right to make shoes, to knit sweaters, in the struggle to plant what one wished, was manifested the natural, indestructible striving toward freedom inherent in human nature. He had seen this very same struggle in the people in camp. Freedom, it seemed, was immortal on both sides of the barbed wire. (Grossman, p. 110)
           </span>
           <nobr>
            
            <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/408153-i-used-to-think-freedom-was-freedom-of-speech-freedom">
             15 likes
            </a>
           </nobr>
           <div class="clear">
           </div>
          </div>
          <a class="actionLink" href="/work/quotes/728857" style="float: right">
           More quotes
          </a>
